REBEL CHIEF
TELLS OF HIS
CAMPAIGNS
Aguinaldo's Account of
War and Flight in
Luzon.
His Plans. Frustrated by the
Sagacity of General
Lawton.
Captured Filipino Leader Speaks of
American Soldiers as Ferocious
Fighters.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
MANILA, April 21. â Tour correspondent
had an Interview this morning -with
Aguinaldo. He was greeted cordially by
the ex-rebel leader, who was in excellent
spirits. Aguinaldo Is still non-committal,
fearing that if he talks on the situation
he will be misrepresented and his posi
tion thus jeopardized. He said:
"I will make no definite statements on
public or private questions until I am
familiar with the situation. I am learn
ing English, and studying American gov
ernment."
When asked if he desired to visit the
United States Aguinaldo replied:
"Tea, greatly; but I am at the disposi
tion of the authorities."
The correspondent then asked him for
his opinion of. the scheme looking: to the
purchase of church estates by the Gov
ernment and their sale to the natives.
Aguinaldo said he thought that such a
scheme would be satisfactory, but ha
added that he was not familiar with the
proposal. He. said he generally endorsed
the acts of the Philippine Commission,
which he had followed Interruptedly in
the newspapers for the last half year. He
had been secluded In the mountains, how
ever, and therefore was not very -well in
formed on current events.
The correspondent presented to him a
photograph of his son, Miguel, which was
taken the day the latter was captured
near San Fabian. Agulnaldo thanked the
correspondent warmly, and said that it
had been reported the boy was dead. He
added, jokingly:
"Whatever else I am accused of, I am
not responsible for the rumors that were
circulated while I was in the field."
. Continuing he said: "I was often very
dose to the Americans. I expected to
make my greatest stand at Calumpit.
"When I abandoned Tarlac I commanded
1500 riflemen. I anticipated General
Wheaton's landing at San Fabian. I
planned to retreat to Nueva Vlscaya, but
was frustrated by brave General Lawton.
I slipped through the cordon with 250
men. only four hours before the landing
party came ashore."
In response to a. auestion concerning
his opinion of the American troops, he
said:
"How terrible are the Americans! They
are splendid and ferocious fighters. I no
sooner built arsenals and barracks than
they destroyed them. Colonel March
chased me in the most lively manner for
two months In the western mountains, un
til I worked eastward with thirty horses
and eighty men. I crossed to Cagayan
and lived on the east coast for eight
months. My outposts often saw the Am
ericans, but I did not participate In a
single engagement, though I once com
manded 40,000 riflemen. The watchfulness
of the army and navy practically de
stroyed filibustering to Luzon.
"I do not desire to discuss Insular poli
tics. I am undecided as to my future
plans. I believe that the Federals will be
strong agents in the pacification of the
archipelago. This Is the first Interview
that I have given to any one."
WELCOME TAJT COMMISSION'.
Leyte Island Natives Give Americans
Hearty Greeting.
TACLOBAN, Island of Leyte. April 21.â
Enthusiastic throngs greeted the appear
ance of the Philippine Commission in the
capital of Leyte. Confidence in American
purposes was reiterated.
Most of the municipalities have been or
ganized, and all were represented at the
conference. The province will be created
to-morrow, with Major Henry Allen of
the Forty-third Volunteer Infantry as
Governor and Lieutenant William S. Con
roy of the same regiment as Treasurer.
Major John S. Gllmore of the Forty
seventh Volunteer Infantry has captured
a high Insurgent, stronghold in Western
Leyte. Lieutenant M. I. Avery of the
same regiment has destroyed another
camp and killed six insurgents.

ABE BUT OF HALF VALUE.
President Opines They Are Worth
Less Than Those at Other
Points.
WASHINGTON, April 21.-In the opin
ion of the President the value of a Filipino
is just one-half that of a person enlisted
in the navy at any other point than the
Philippine Islands. This Idea of the in
feriority of the natives of the Philippine*
appears In a general order Issued by the
Navy Department publishing an executive
order establishing, an insular force of thÂ»
United States navy and designating rat
ings and rate's of pay. Therein this force
13 limited to five hundred and will con
sist of cockswains, seamen, ordinary sea

stewards, cooks and mess attendant*.

Viceroy To Orders the Capture and
WILL CRUSH ROBBER BANDS.
Execution of Bandits.
TACOMA, April 21.â To-day's mall ad
vices from Hongkong give details of en
ergetic preparations being made by Vice
roy To at Canton to capture and execute
robbera and bandits throughout tho two
Kwang provinces.,* For years bandits and
pirates have practically ruled Southern
China. They have become much bolder
during the disturbances in Northern
China until the officials were powerless to
cope with them. Viceroy To was appoint
ed at the special instance of U Hung
Chang and directed to employ all his
power to .kijl off the rohbera- and bring
about a restoration of peaceful trade.
Viceroy To declares that bad characters
in the villages and towns are undoubtedly
well known to the gentry- He therefore
orders the gentry in each town to imme
diately prepare lists of the names of all
doubtful persons and present them with
out delay to the local mandarins. It is
ordered that as fast as any person on
these lists is caught In any wrongdoing
he shall ' be arrested and executed. To.
encourage soldiers to do their duty,' com
pensation will be granted those who are
wounded and their families will be pen
sioned in case of death. Large sums are
promised to Informers and a free pardon
to any robber who is instrumental in
bringing three notorious robbers to jus
tice. As many robbers are known as be
ing men of substance, it is agreed that
their property shall be divided among in
formers, Villages failing to comply with
the Viceroy's orders are threatened with
destruction!. Soldiers are warned against
using their position to extort money.
Viceroy To estimates that at least 3000
bandits will be executed under these or
ders this summer.
A correspondent of the Hongkong Mail
writes from Hoihow that 18,000 persons
are reported to have died of plague dur
ing the last three months in Lamko dis
trict of the island of Hainan. By the ex
ercise of a strict quarantine the plague
has been kept out bf Hoihow. The entire
population is stricken in the district
named.
Ninety junior judges and public pro
curators have resigned in Japan as a result
of the failure of the Diet to pass a bill
increasing their salaries. The matter has
assumed the form of a strike, the judges
of Osaka. and Toklo declining to act fur
ther unless the Government Increases
their stipend. They now receive 500 to
600 yen annually.

LONDON. April 21.â The echoes of the
London and Globe liquidation are fitfully
heard in the vicinity of Lombard street
and Lothbury, where official liquidators
have been engaged for weeks past on the
Herculean task of clearing up accounts,
a task which threatens to outlive even
the capture of Dewct.
Some of the stories related by Whitaker
Wright to-night at the last meeting of the
shareholders are spoken of now as akin
to old Â¦ nursery rhymes told to children.
For instance, the yarn about wicked con
spirators who sought to corrupt a mine
manager with a bribe of Â£100.000, but
whose machinations were just nipped be
fore, fruition by the unfortunate collapse
of the London and Globe, is now regarded
as fiction of the weakest kind. The much
vaunted legal action against the treacher
ous Lake View syndicate, which sold out
right under the nose of the directors of
the London and Globe, Is also pigeon
holed as among things no longer men
tioned.
Meantime there departed yesterday by
the Lucania Heniy Bratnober of San
Francisco and a few friends who have
no reason to complain of their connection
with Whitaker Wright. It is understood
that this combination, which operated on
the bear side of the London and Globe
group, cleared a round million sterling by
the operation.
In London the anticipated advent of J.
Pierpont Morgan has been signalized by
dealings in American Industrial stocks.
Since it has become customary in Eng
land to speak of the great Wall-street
magnate as a steel trust potentate it nat
urally followed that the first dealings in
Americanvlndustrials were all in steel
trust sharp. To be perfectly candWrhcw-
ever, it is only fair to state that the deal
ings in these" stocks were limited, as they
were mostly confined to an American cli
entele.
For the steel 7 per cent Dref erred $98
was freely offered when outside trading
began, all of the trading being, of course,
on the curb, owing to the fact that the
stock has not yet been listed on the Lon
don Stock Exchange. But the supply
proved too much for the applicants and
those who came late were fortunate in
paying only $96 for the stock.
The difficulty of trading in this, as in
all American industrial stocks, is that
the British speculative public cannot un
derstand how shares can spring Into ex
istence without a chance of making a pre
mium of the first allotment. If the Bank
of England were to -Issue fully paid or
fully underwritten stock, even 7 per cent
preferred, British parsons and old maids
who keenly scent a prospective premium
would hardly apply for a pound's worth.
Henry Bratnober, who, together with a
few friends, is credited with having
cleared Â£1,000,000 through the collapse of
the London and Globe Corporation, Is
well known and very popular at the Pal
ace Hotel, where he and his wife resided
shortly before his departure for the scene
of his latest successes. Mrs. Bratnober
did not accompany h:r husband to Eng
land, but took up her residence in Pied
mont, where she is at present.

VANCOUVER. Wash.. April 21.-Charles
Brcwn and E. L. Canby, president and
cashier respectively of the First Xatlonal
Bank of Vancouver, which was closed
yesterday by the Controller of the Cur
rency, committed suicide last night two
railes from this city by shooting them
selves with a revolver.
The bodies of the men were found this
morning lying together In a small clump
of bushes about a half mile north of the
Columbia School, which Is situated on the
outskirts of the town. They both used
the same weapon, and Canby evidently
died first, as the revolver was found in
Hrcwn's hand. It is evident that Canby
put the muzzle of the revolver in his
mouth and then blew the top of his head
off. Brown then took it and shot him
self In exactly the same way, falling over
Canby's body.
The fact that Brown's bicycle and an
Â¦umbrella belonging to Canby were found
a. short distance out of town last night led
to the belief that the two bank officials
nad taken their lives. Early this morning
a searching party started out, and after
several hours' hunt the bodies were found
Jn the clump of bushes.
First Attempt at Suicide.
Friday evening Canby. upon being told
Â¦by Eank Examiner J. W. Maxwell that
the bank would not be permitted to open
Saturday, went out In the yard behind
the bank and attempted to shoot himself.
His revolver failed to explode, although
all five chambers were loaded. After fail
ing to shoot himself Canby came back
into the bank and he and President Brown
left together, the latter taking his revol
ver. When Examiner Maxwell confronted
the bank officials with the shortage of
JSI.900 which he had discovered both men
admitted their guilt. It is said that
Brcwn and Canby had been speculating
In stocks. Besid* Brown's body there was
found a package containing Â£5 and a note,
saying that the money belonged to his
daughter. Upon Canby's body the follow
ing brief note was found:
"My dear wife: 1 feel what I am about
to do is for the brst. Forgive me If you
can and try to live for our dear children.
God bless you all. Good-by.
"NED.

Brown, who was 54 years of age, had
lived in Vancouver since 1862. His father
was the first Receiver of the Vancouver
I^and Office. He has served as Auditor
of. Clark County, was married and has a
family. His connection with the bank as
its president dates back ten years.
Canby was C2 years old and was well
connected. His wife was Miss Frances
Burnside, daughter of an Oregon pioneer.
He has a brother, who is a paymaster in
the United States army. Another brother
committed suicide by Jumping off. the
wharf at Vancouver several years ago.
Eighty-One Thousand Missing.
While Bank Examiner Maxwell, now
in chare* as receiver, has made no offi
cial statement, it is known that the bank
has loaned a large sum of money on in

JSO.OOn. At that time about one-half of

Fine* that time.
The heaviest stockholder is Colonel
Stephen P. Joslyn of the regular army.
Colonel Joslyn is the mustering-out officer
at San Francisco. His post was Vancou
ver when the bank was organized In 1SS5.
end ever since that time he has been the
heaviest stockholder. According to the
last statement Issued In February the
bank had deposits amounting to about

The Â¦VTestln^house Klectric Company
had two carloads of finished material on
the tracks near Turtle Creek. They were
caught in the flood and water got into the
cars. The loss may reach $80,000.
On the Fort Wayne the worst trouble
was a snow blockade between Salem and
Massillon. This tied up the road for twen
ty-four hours. The same trouble kept the
Cleveland trains of the Pittsburg and
Lake Erie late, five trains having been
blockaded at Windom, near Levittsburg,
for twenty-five hours.
At Skobo the Lake Erie received its
most serious landslide. One hundred feet
of westbound track were carried down the
Fleep bank, tho whole face of the bank
Flipping- down into the river. On the lines
of the Pennsylvania and Baltimore and
Ohio, to Wheeling, great damage is re
ported.
The damage to telegraph poles and lines
along all the roads was enormous, and it
will take several days to fully repair
them. Trains are now running on all
roads, but no attention is paid to keeping
up to schedule.
The submerged districts in Pittsburg
and Allegheny are to-day a scene of abject
misery- The residents spent all day try
ing to restore their homes to some degree
of decency and comfort. The damage to
furniture and building's in Allegheny is es
timated at about $100,000. It will take two
months cf hot summer weather to thor
oughly dry out the houses. In Pittsburg
the loss to residences and stores and
goods and the cost of cleaning up will
amount to about $250,000.
At Carnegieborough, six miles from
Pittsburg, the !oss is estimated at $40,000,
of which the Columbia Bridge Works lose
$5000.
While there have been greater floods at
this point, there was â¢ never one that
caused so mucn financial loss and discom
fort. The loss to the railroads entering
Pittsburg from flood, landslides, wrecked
bridges, heavy tnow and the interference
with traffic is roughly estimated at
% 1.000,000.

M1DDLET0WN, N. Y., April 21.-Mrs.
Maria Conkling, wife of ! David Conkling
of Rockhlll, who died suddenly on Thurs
day, had a premonition of her fate and
foretold her death, having been warned in
dreams on three successive nights that her
end was near. The facts in the case,
which are authenticated by members of
her family and by neighbors to whom she
related her dreams while making prepara
tions for the death which she was con
vinced was near, are as follows:
Mrs. Conkling was 49 years old and-ap
parently in perfect health. On Monday
night she dreamed that her daughter, who
died twelve years ago, appeared to her
and beckoned to her; on Tuesday night
her mother, who died about twenty-five
years ago, appeared to her while she slept
and beckoned to her. . She told . these
dreams to her family and was much dis
turbed by them. On Wednesday night she
dreamed that a black-robed figure of
death stood by her bedside holding a taper
and, while it beckoned^ the taper suddenly
went out.
She interpreted the visions as meaning
that death was- near and that it would be
sudden, and made preparations. That af
ternoon, while engaged in household
duties, the final summons came in the
form of a stroke of apoplexy. . '^'

Some Asked-For Changes. .
WASHINGTON, April 21.â It Is not like
ly that the new Philippine tariff will be
promulgated until after the decision of the
Supreme Court in the insular case. It was
said at the War Department, where the
case has received considerable attention,
that this decision may make it unneces
sary for this Government to establish, a
system of tariff, rates in the Philippines,
although the department has proceeded
with the work of equalizing the rates and
considering suggestions- as though the
coming decision would not make any
change in the present conditions.
A change Is asked In the classification
of meat products. It is said that the duty
on alcohol should be doubled, or manu
facturers of whisky will be able to use the
alcohol at a less cost than the distilled
product.
California manufacturers ask a reduc
tion on flour, cereala, prunes, benzoin,
gasoline and retro!eum, some -of them
suggesting that these articles be placed on
the free list. Condensed milk and canned
fish the Pacific desires should also be
placed on the free list.
One suggestion Â¦ made \s that all mate
rials for the United States forces should
be admitted free of duty.
Some rather Interesting information has
reached the War Department In connec
tion with the tariff, it being shown that
Russia sends to the Philippines two-fifths
of all the petroleum Imported by the peo
ple of the islands.
Reductions are asked of from 20 to 30 per
cent on harness and saddleroakers' wares.
It is suggested that cheap and expensive
machinery should not pay the same rates.
Certain typewriter firms want the duty on
their machines reduced.
Alcohol Rate Too Low.
The dairy Interest believes a provision
desirable. A lower rate Is asked for. gaso
line to be used for fuel and for launches.
An advalorum duty is advised upon pre
cious stone and watches. One suggestion
is that copperas and oxide should be upon
the free list.
It is claimed that cotton yarn should not
be admitted at a lower rate than fabrics
woven from such yarn; also that tho
weight and rating of cotton tissues
should be changed In the interest of coarser
cotton . fabrics. There has been a quite
extensive discussion of this subject, and
it has been pointed out that changes in
the Cuban tariff also were needed in this
particular.

New Haven Fears Awful Disaster

NEW HAVEN, Conn., April 2Lâ Reports
from around the State show that the
damage done by the floods is very serious
The worst situation Is at Winsted. where
Highland Lake threatens an overflow al
most any hour. If such occurs it is feared
a terrible loss of life and property will
ensue.
The Connecticut River at Hartford is
rising at the rate of an inch an hour. The
lower portion of - the city . Is completely
under water, and on several streets boats
have been called into service.

CINCINNATI, April 21.â There has

â¢ snows and rains along the Ohio "Valley.

received from all over Southern Ohio and
as far north as Springfield. Trains are
late on all railroads. It is generally con
ceded that, the fruit crop throughout the
Ohio Valley is gone, and the damage to

BIG LAKE AT BEAVER FALLS.
Body of Water Occupies th.e Place of
North End Squares.
PITTSBURG, Pa., April 21,-The report
to-night from Rochester is that the Ohio
has overflowed its banks and a consider
able portion of the city lying south of
the Fort Wayne Railroad track is sub
merged. The river is still rising, and a
steady downpour of rain continues. The
loss to manufacturing plants along- the
river will.be heavy. At Beaver Falls, Pa.,
several sauares in the north end of the
town were converted into a lake. Reports
from up river points! received at Pittsburg
up to 10 p. m. are meager, but for the
most part are encouraging.
The storm left a zone of ruin 200 miles in
diameter.
Some municipalities are in dire straits
from rain. Traffic has been practically
suspended. Water overflowed and washed
out railroad tracks and hillsides, and
came down and buried the rails. In ad
dition, nearly every town- on the Ohio
River between Pittsburg and Wheeling is
in darkness to-night, owing to damaged
wires. '
The money loss is difficult to foot up.
It may reach IS.OOO.OOO. 1 The telegraph
companies have suffered severely.' There
is an enormous indirect damage caused
by suspension of trade.
FIERCE STORMS OF SNOW BLOCK
EASTERN MAIL TRAIN TRAVEL
Telegraph Lines Go Down, Gars Are Stalled
and in Greater New York-Sweepers Sent to
Clean the Streets Get Mired in the Slush
NEW YORK, April 2Lâ Because of the
heavy storm which has been raging in the
East all traffic into Cleveland has been de
layed from two to twelve hours. The
Grand Central depot presented a strange
appearance this afternoon, as the belated
Western trains-arrived several hours late,
with their roofs and steps encrusted with"
enow and ice. Friends of the long-due
passengers hung around the depot all day
and rushed to meet the weary passengers
every time a train came in. The disap
pointments were frequent, as most of the
trains were divided into sections at Buf
falo.
The Atlantic express, which carries all
the mail east of Chicago, and which was
due at 5:30 a. ni., arrived seven hours be
hind time 1 . The New York and New Eng
land express, due at 2:55 p. m., arrived in
two sections, the first section being two
hours and twenty minutes late and the
second section five hours late. The first
section brought with it a Lake Shore train
from the West due at 1:30 p. m. Most of
the Western trains on the Pennsylvania
were also far behind their schedule time.
Oreat difficulty -was experienced in petting
the trains through east of Pittsburg.
At the postoffice this afternoon it was
said that all mail matter east of San
Francisco was 'ate. The 4:45 a, m. train,
one of the fastest in the service, carrying
all the Northwestern mail east of San
Francisco, did not get in until 11 a. m.,
makim? it six hours and forty-five minutes
late. The New York and Dunkirk train on
the Erie, carrying all the New York State
mail, due at 6:30 a. m., had not arrived up
to 3:30 p. m. Most of the other minor
trains carrying mail were also late.
Considerable property was destroyed
along West street in this city, owing to
the flooding of cellars. Large quantities
of perishable produce was stored in the
cellars along: 'his street and : much of it
was destroyed by the water, which, in
many places, was waist deep. In several
of the old-fashioned houses where there
are no cellars the water came above the
flooring and office furniture could be seen
floating around the stores on the ground
floor.

WIN OPENING MATCH
Winners of Last Year's Pennant De-
William .Muller, a barkeeper employed
by I>. Susing, Twelfth and Franklin
streets, Oakland, shot himself through
the heart to-night in the room occupied
by his wife at 533 Hobart street. Muller
was married last August, but had been
separated from his wife some time. Re
cently Mrs. Muller brought suit for di
vorce, and to-night Muller went to ,the
Hobart-street house to see her.
Just what' passed between the two at
this last interview Mrs. Muller declines
to state, but it is probable that the bar
keeper made threats against his wife's
life, as they had been together but a few
minutes when she suddenly screamed and
Jumped through the .window on to the
porch. Muller killed himself a moment
later. - Â¦' i
Muller left a note saying that If any
thing happened to him J. E. McElroy, his
attorney, would look after his affairs, and
this fact, together with Mrs. Muller's leap
through Â¦ the window, leads the police to
believe that. Muller contemplated killing
the woman and then committing suicide.

ETAHTORD DOES NOT
GET A SINGLE SET
California gave Stanford a terrible drub
bing on the California tennis courts Sat
urday in the tenth annual intercollegiate
tennis tournament. It was the most sig
nal victory either college has won since
intercollegiate tennis was started.
\ Several hundred people were on hand to
encourage the collegians ' when play was
called. Both singles events were played
at the same time, but the one between
Harry Wiehe and Paul Selby attracted
the most attention. At the start the for
mer tried to. play the net game, but ho
soon realized that that would not do, as
Selby was passing him continually with
both back and fore hand drives. Selby 1 s
accuracy in placing his â¢ drives was re
markable, and his opponent seemed to
lose confidence very rapidly as the game
progressed. Wiehe, whose forte is vol
leying, was very weak at the net. Selby.
played by far the best game he has ever
put up, but Wiehe was a trifle off in his
play. The complete score in favor of
Selby was 6â2, &â 3, 6â3. . .
While Selby was disposing of Wlehe
Drummoiid McGavin was administering
an equally crushing defeat to William
Roth, Stanford's second man- in singles.
McGavin, who is usually very nervous in
a tournament, was perfectly cool through
out tho match and executed his long,
sweeping drives to perfection. Roth made
a good stand in the second set, pulling it
up from 3â5 to 5â5, only to lose 'in the
end, Sâ 6. McGavin outplayed his oppo
nent at every point of the game, and his
smashing Â¦ was a feature. The score ln
McGavin's favor was 6â1, 8â6, 6â3. :
Henry Wlehe and Dick Adams, the fa
mous pony team, were generally picked to
win the doubles, but the California men
showed that they can play doubles fully
as well as singles. "With the tennis cham
pionship already won the California men
were very confident and took many
chances, while the . Stanford men were
discouraged and played on the defensive
throughout. Adams played a line .game,
but Wlehe was very much disheartened
and did not support his partner as well
as usual. McGavin and Selby played an
aggressive game from start to finish.
Both men drove splendidly from the back
court and up. at the net killed. everything.
The Stanford men resorted to a lobbing
game, which did not prove very effective
against their hard-hitting opponents. The
first set : was very â¢ close, but after that
everything went Berkeley's way and she
won the next two sets rather . easily.
Adams played a very gritty game and
tried hard to stave off defeat, but to no
avail. The score in favor of -Selby and
McGavin, the California men, was 7â5,
&-3, 6-3.
Of the ten intercollegiate tennis tour
naments played each college has won five.

BUTTE, Mont., April 21.â A special from
Chestnut, Mont., to the Miner says: Henry
H. Morrlck, a 7-year-old grandson of Hen
ry Harrison, a well-known rancher, was

youngster entered the granary to drive
out some chickens and while he was doing

force of the fall causing the -veapon to be
discharged. The contents struck the little
fellow, killing him instantly.

Plans Conservative Beform.
Perhaps the most Important feature of
the document is its general presentation
of, the view that the time is now at hand
for China to adopt plans of conservative
Wu Is a. loval and devoted admirer' of
this country and its institutions, and he
has not been in sympathy with past ef
forts for excessive change, as in his judg
ment they went so far as to invite pre
judice and opposition, not only in im
perial and other ruling quarters, but also
with the mass of the peop" , who are slow
to realize the advantage of sweeping
changes. In the present Instance he has
sought to find a middle ground of con
servative Improvement which would com
mend itself both to the Chinese Govern
ment and to the world at large as calcu
lated to remove tho most serious obsta
cles la tha way of progress.
In determining upon this course. Minis
ter Wu is acting entirely upon* his own
Initiative and responsibility and without
any preference to hla status aÂ» Minister
to Washington or on any suggestion from
official* here. While he has had the mat
ter \n contemplation for eiome time, yet
there has been no occasion or opportun
ity for giving expression to his plans.
Now, however, this opportunity ha* pre
vented itaelt In tho form ot u recent Im
perial edict invittne tho hleh otttclal* ot
China Â«t homo and abroadâ Viceroy*.
Oovernow ami Mlnlaters abroadâ to pre
sent to the Government every facility at
their command to afd In overcoming the
invfMMii tllÂ«MHtrouft condition of ufralr*.
The edict points out past evIU and hold*
in severe account the members of the old
regime whose conduct has been largely
responsible for the distress through which
the country htÂ»Â« passed. The Government
therefore asks for every aid which Its
patriotic cltl*cns can bring to It In over
coming the evils of the past and In estab
lishing a'secure basis for the future. Act
ing upon this edict Mr. Wu has prepared
his comprehensive memorial to the Gov
ernment. A memorial of this character,
It is said, Is the most important act a
Chinese official can take, for it Is not re
sorted to except on rare occasions when
the gravity of a cause warrants an offi
cial to comeilnto immediate communica
tion with the imperial authorities on high
questons of state.
People Slow to Realize.
He has prepared a comprehensive docu
ment, covering twenty heads dealing with
administrative, economic and financial
questions, and taking In practically the
entire range of Government afflairs so far
as there seems to be at present oppor
tunity of rehabilitating them on modern
and progressive lines. The document is,
however, most conservative In character
and purposely ayolds radicalism and ex
treme reforms Vvhich have interfered
with previous efforts to bring about a
change in the existing order of affairs.
CHICAGO, April 21.â The . Chinese Mlnr
ister, Wu Ting Fang:, is about to make
a move of far-reaching Importance to the
affairs of China and one which' cannot.
It is believed, but engage the favorable
attention of all the foreign powers inter
ested in the affairs of the Chinese Gov
ernment. It is based upon his Intimate
acquaintance with the affairs of the west
ern world and points out wherein there
Is the opportunity for China to adopt an
enlightened and progressive policy, which
will brins it into harmony with the sis
terhood of nations and at the same time
will relieve it from the present perplexi
ties which are crowding upon It at all

WOODLAND. April 21.-Followinjr the
meeting of the executive committee of the

Saturday afternoon was a public meeting
which lasted into the night and which
was well attended by members of B.oards
of Trade, Chambers of Commerce. Super
visors and other organizations of the
twelve counties comprising the associa
tion.
The election of officers of the associa
tion for the ensuing year resulted as fol
lows: President, W. S. Green: secretary,
W. C. Wright; treasurer, Frank Miller.
Vice presidents were elected as follows:
J. J. Chambers, Shasta; J. F. Foster Te
hama; R. M. Green, Butte; Colonel
Forbes. Yuba: H. J. Stabler. Sutter- "W J
Englebright, Placer; J. O. Coleman, Sac
ramento: P. R. Garnett. Glenn; Colusa,
J. W. Kaeurth; C. W. Thomas, Yolo; R.
Burcur, Soluno.
A motion prevailed that it was the
sense of the meeting that the twelve
counties in the association send one rep
resentative to the Pan-American Exposi
tion to be held in Buffalo, and that each
of the twelve counties be requested to fur
nish for distribution literature setting
forth its resources.
President Green, Secretary Wright and
J. O. Coleman were appointed a com
mittee to forward to members of the
boards of supervisors of the twelve
counties copies of the president's annual
report, also a letter containing estimates
of the cost of maintaining the association
another year, and of keeping a renresen
tative at Buffalo durins the exposition
with an urgent request that each of the
counties make an appropriation for that
purpose.
The executive committee of the asso
ciation will meet in Vacavllle on the third
Saturdav in May.

SANTA BARBARA, April 21.â A freight
train on the coast line, leaving here at 10
o'clock this morning, was wrecked at
Summerland. about six miles south of this
city. The trucks of one of the cars
about the middle of the train left tho
rails and before the train could be
stopped six cars had been piled upon the
tracks, all badly broken. No one was In
jured, the crew being in the caboose,
which did not leave the rails. A wreck
ing train has been sent for to clear the
tracks. The rails and ties are torn up for
a distance of 300 yards. Passengers and
baggage on Incoming trains are being
transferred around the wreck.

One Man Must Reign.
The plan of the powers, as recently made
known in dispatches from Peking, Is that
the old board of ten members shall be
replaced by a new board of five members,
with a Prince of the blood at it9 head, two
Ministers of Foreign Affairs and two Vice
Ministers, the latter to have a knowledge
of foreign languages. This plan has not
been wholly satisfactory to the foreign
Ministers and their Governments, but
they have accepted Jt with the belief that
it will be at least an Improvement on tha
old Tsunc-U Yamen. and one* which woxikl
be more likely to concur with China's de
sire to have Important work performed
by boards Instead of Individuals.
reform, i In stating the need and ajrere
gate of this the Minister's memorial will
point to the lesson Japan-has given to the
Â¦world. Up to a few years ago the condi
tions In China and Japan were almost
Identical. But by the gradual adoption
of modern methods of procedure Japan
has brought- herself Into close communi
cation with the rest of the world, and
now occupies an enviable position among
the other recognized great powers. Min
ister Wu holds that there Is nothing lack
ing In the resources of China or in the
character of her people to keep her from
securing the same advantages that have
come to Japan. The memorial does not.
however, confine itself to generalization,
but specifies along what lines changes
can be made with advantage. As stated,
these cover administrative, economic and
financial . conditions. In some respects,
also, Mr. Wu's recommendations will take
up lines of reform that have been brought
forward by the powers m the negotiations
at Peking, and will point out that instead
of resisting: them it will be to the ad
vantage of China to go even further than
the powers have suggested. For, Instance,
one of the most important subjects re
cently under consideration has been that
relating to the establishment of & for
eign office at Peking to take the place ot
the Tsung-11 Yamen.

THE COMING YACHT RACE.
SIR THOMAS LIPTON'S yacht, Shamrock II,
challenger for the America's cup, has been suc
cessfully launched amid. the plaudits of a crowd
that expect her lo be. a winner.. The Countess of Duf
ferin has broken a bottle ofwine over the prow and
the naming has been duly done. The day. was fair,
the workmen of the yard in which the yacht was built
took a holiday. Everything tended to make the occa
sion a joyous one and to fill the British with sanguine
expectations of victory. When Sir Thomas was asked
whether, if beaten, he would try again, he replied: â¢â¢"I
expect next year to build a defender, not a challenger."
While everything is thus harmonious, and -merry
in the camp. of the challengers, there is stuTgoing on
among the defenders a controversy that never should
have arisen. .: It is the now well-worn wrangle over the
question whether right of. defending the cup is open
to all American yachtsmen, or only to those of the
New York Yacht Club. â¢" : ".'
Thomas W. Lawson of Boston, in company with
other New England yachtsmen, has had constructed
a yacht which all loyal New Englanders believe to be
much better than anything the New Yorkers can con
struct. They claim the right to compete with the
yachts of the New Yorkclub, and hold that if success
ful, their boat' shall havp the privilege , of defending
the cup against 'the British" challenger. That right
has been disputed. " The New .York Yacht Club claims
the exclusive right to defend the cup, .and: it appears
it' is in a position to enforce the claim. ,
' It has been suggested that Lawson might arrange
the contest amicably by placing his . boat nominally
in the hands of a. member of the New York club, but
to that suggestion he is reported to have recently
replied: "In' regard to the statements printed that
under certain conditions I would resort to the subter
fuge of allowing some one else, who had no owner
ship in my. boat, to be proclaimed her owner for. the
sake of being allowed to race, I can only say I regret
Â¦there is any one connected with yachting so unmanly
as-to think this possible. No one has ever suggested
such a thing to .me'.- Rather than resort to the methods
suggested to obtain a race I would, regret,
sink the Independence on her launching day."
Thus the matter stood at the time of the latest. report
given to the public of the negotiations. Technically
tfcc New York-Yacht .Club is right, but it is safe to
say'few Americans will approve of such a stand taken
upon a technicality. % It is desirable that the yacht race
becarried out between the best boat, in America and
the, best of any other nation. .-The defense should not
be confined to a^ single club, ; and -.in their efforts-to
force a recognition -of their yacht the. Bosten men
have the sympathy of the country.^ . ;
ART AND H'STORY.
rpvOMANCE has long smceffreed itself-; from the
L_y trammels of histotic accuracy, and consequently
* * when an imaginative writer sits down to con
struct an historical .novel he feels .at; liberty to 'deal
with great personages as he 'cho_oses. He may group
them in whatever way best splits the dramatic require-'
ments of his story, and,; indeed, take /almost Â¦ any
other privilege he desires. That much is conceded;
but has a sculptor the same right as a romancer? Has
an artist who is engaged to design a public monu
ment a privilege to change the facts of history in" or
der to make a striking ] group of illustrious men?
That is a question which is now agitating the East. .
The discussion has arisen over one of the bronze
tablets that adorns the pedestal of the recently un
veiled monument to General Logan at Washington.
It appears that for the sake of surrounding Logan
with some of the most distinguished men of his time
the sculptor has represented him as taking the oath
of office as Senator standing amid a circle of famous
statesmen who were never gathered on any such
occasion. â¢ 'Â¦)
i *The. Chicago Inter, Ocean presents the facts thus:
"This relief represents Vice -President Arthur admin
istering the oath ;to Senator Logan in the presence
Â¦'of Senators Conkling, Cullom, Evarts, Morton, Voor-
r hees, Thurman and Miller of California. General
Logan's first term in the Senate began March 4, 1871,
and ended March 3, 1877. Â¦ He entered upon his sec
ond term March 18, tS/9, during the extra session.
Mr. Arthur first presided over the Senate at the extra
session beginning March 4, 1881, and never admin
istered the oath to Senator Logan. Senator. Cullom
did not enter the Senate until December. 4, 188,3.
Senator Evarts was not. elected until 1885. Senator
Morton died. November , 1, 1877. He probably saw
Logan sworn in in 1871, but the oath was administered
by Vice President Coif ax. Senator Miller was not
elected until January, i88i.' Senators Conkling, Voor
hees and- Thurman were in the Senate when Logan
was sworn 'by Vice President Wheeler, but Thurman
appears not to have been present on that occasion.
Thus it appears that only so much of the scene as rep
resents Logan "taking the, oath, with Conkling and
Voorhees as witnesses, has any historic truth."
It is asserted that the sculptor, Franklin Simmons,
claims that he selected !the\Senators to be" placed- in
the group at the suggestion of Mrs. Logan. . .- That
excuse, however, even if it be : vajid,. would; not affect
the main question of the right of any one to violate
history in the construction' of : an 'historic monument,
1101. has it counted for much with the supporter's of
either side of the controversy.' It is to ~ be; noted,
therefore, merely as .in;."explahatidri: of, the 1 sculptor's
motive, and not as a defense of the act itself.
I All classes of artists must have privileges, sculptors
>as well as noveli^s, but it would seem that in this case,
the privilege has been carried too far. A distinction
should be made between art designed solely for ar
tistic purposes and a work designed mainly for his
toric purposes. A public monument erected for the
object of commemorating a great figure in' the his
tory of the country should be strictly true to history
or else frankly ideal and symbolical. There can be no
objection to David's great picture of Napoleon
. crossing the Alps on a fiery warhorse, because every
I one who sees the picture perceives at once the essen-
I tinl idealism of the hero as there presented; but
.when called upon to paint the coronation of Napo
[leon David Had to deal with an historic event, and
In a recent address Senator- Platt of Connecticut
stated that since the close of the war over 40,000 per
sons hare'emigrated from Spain or. the Canary Islands
to Cuba, so it is evident the Spanish people do not
believe American interference has injured Cuba's pros
pects to any great extent.
The story that San Francisco has not enough hovel
room to furnish lodging for a guest who comes lats,
is a good story in its >vÂ£.y, but all* the same no one
need stay away from thecityj'on that account. When
the crowds come here to greet the President Sp.n
Francisco hospitality' will find a means of providing
them vvith all "sorts of accommodations. .
THE BOOM IN WALL STREET.
AS far as figures go last week was almost a record
breaker in trade. Certainly an enormous
amount of money changed hands in business.
Ihe clearings of the .country, showed the remarkable
increase of 76.6 per cent over last year, the. gain at
New York being 100.8 per cent, at Boston 73.4 per
c*nt and af Pittsburg 56.3 per cent. Every important
ciiv in the country showed a gain.
All this is brilliant, and to the general observer in
dicates intense activity in business throughout the
United States. But to the shrewd and posted financier,
vho keeps close to the center of affairs and knows
what is going on, the glittering figures wear another
aspect. It is this: The major part of this great gain
in the bank clearings and apparent volume of trade
is confined to the thre; cities mentioned above. In
other words the great steel and railroad trusts are
causing most of the commotion, chiefly in Wall street.
The gain in business as represented by the clearings,
is speculative rather than real, on paper rather than
consumptive. New Yoik and Boston are the cities
wncre the coin is kept and where all the big schemes
are financed, and Pittsburg is the city where the steel
works are. To . be still more explicit, the immense
steel corporation, lately formed with a capitalization
of over $1,000,000,006, and which is the biggest thing
hi its line that the world has even seen,' has started
into motion gigantic wheels which are now revolving
over an enormous area and making everything around
them dizzy. If the stsei corporation -were eliminated
from the commercial proposition it would be seen
that while the country is prosperous, with its treasury
full of gold and a good condition of trade everywhere,
it is not really making the gain in general trade repre
sented by that 76.6 per cent for the country and that
100.8 per cent at New York. â¢
As for Wall street, it was a wild place last Friday.
Ail previous stock market records were broken, and
the recorded sales amounted to over 2,220,000 shares,
to say nothing of the ether thousands of shares sold
en the outside. Enormous holdings changed hands
in single blocks, and if there" were any bears in Wall
street they were in their holes or covering at rising
prices. . .
The old heads in Will street are wondering when
all this going to stop, and how. For that matter,
they have been wondering for a year or more. Most
c! them expected that it would come to an end long
before this, but there seems to be as much steam as
ever. /
As hinted above, general trade does not exhibit
any particular steam. Cold, snowy and rainy weather
has interfered with the distributive trade in the West
and Noithwest, and checked a good deal of cotton
planting in the South, as shown by the cancellation
oi large orders for fertilizers used in the cotton fielJs.
The Georgia mills are running time, but many
other mills have made material reductions. Wool con
tinues dull, but the feeling is steady. The large manu
facturers are not doing much, but the small ones are.
buying moderately. The shipments of boots and
shoes from Eastern points are steadily falling off.
though they are still ahead of those a year ago. Hides
ere reported firmer at Chicago, but leather is weaker
and boots are lower. Piovisions have not been as
firm, though hogs are still scarce. Against these
wlher unfavorable signs, however, we have increased
railroad earnings, showing a large movement in gen
cia! merchandise, a large distribution of spring and
summer goods, easy collections and an enormous
amount of gold in the national treasury. As for crop
conditions, they are i^niarkably favorable. But the
iron and steel trade continues to lead everything else
in sight. Immense quantities are wanted for bridges,
buildings and farming implements, and plans are be
ing drawn for new mills of enormous capacity.
This is the commercial situation throughout the
country at large. In our local market there is noth
ing new to report, con Jition^ remaining about as t'cv
have been given for several months. Our local .crop
prospects are satisfactory, and while the country over
the other side of the Rockies continues to flourish we
are all right.

Novelists Are Numerous.
JUtogether there are at least fifty novel
ists of some pretensions in Japan, and
most of them find subsistence by writing
serials for the newspapers and turning
out, as from a mill, humorous or sensa
tional paragraphs. Only two or three out
of all these can earn enough money to
keep themselves respectable. Oxakl Koyo,
already mentioned, and one of the most
prominent of the group, was born in ISto.
and. after spending three years in the
Tokio Imperial University, entered on his
career in literature. He is a combination
of Zola and Henry James, though far in
ferior to f lther. He is praised for his del
icate touch and graceful style; and has a
predilection for delineating the sunny side
of life. He has a. theoryâ sarcastic per
sons say that It's not an unselfish theoryâ
that state aid should be given to' authors,
and defends that theory with a warmth
that has lately called forth indignant re
plies from various quarters. On being told
once that Zola had spent a whole week in
studying character in tram cars, he was
filled with envy. "Happy," he exclaimed,
"is the author, who is so little pressed by
the production of moneymaking books aa
to be able to devote so much time to
trifling affairs like that." His principal
works are "Three Wives." "Dimples on
the Cheek." "Pillow of Incense Wood.''
'Love Sick" and "Darkness of Heart.'
Their names vividly suggest the subjects
with which they all deal. ~ '
" Koda Roban is of about the same age as
Koyo and was once a clerk in a telegraph
office. He was regarded In his youth as
the blockhead of the family. This block
head is now regarded by the Japanese a3
their Meredith. Hid works are esthetic
and are celebrated . for their wondrous
nights of imagination, but his style Is fre
quently abrupt and incomprehensible to
ordinary readers. He Is.- in fact, like
Meredith and Jamesâ a writer for the cul
tured, his audience being select, if few.
and . most of his works . treat of some
master" artisf entirely "absorbed tn his
work and alike Indifferent to the doings
and -the; opinions of the- busy outside
world."- Â¦â¢â¢ Â¦Â¦ -â¢ Â¦'"-Â¦Â¦
The Rqban. family Is a remarkable one.

A Japanese Thomas Hardy.
Hirotsu Ryuro, another of the con
temporary novelists of Japan, is four or
five years older than either of the two
preceding writers, and Is regarded by
the Japanese as their. Thomas Hardy. He
is fond of tragedies In common life, and
his peasant characters, as well as his
plots, are regarded in this country with
great admiration. He Is even more oro-
Jiflc4han JCoyo,, and^possesaes jnore of a
business spirit than most of Tils con
freres â a fact due, perhaps, to the circum
stance that he was once a petty official in
the Government service. His "Love-Sui
cide at Imado," in which a de
graded woman dies with a man
whom she does not love. Is 'famous
for the masterly way in which he
traces the gradual development of his
characters. Another famous work of his
is "The Tragedy of Kawachlya." in which
a virtuous wife Dines away and dies on
account of her husband's infatuation for
a tea-house woman, who is not in love
with him. however, but with his brother.
The brother's rejection of the woman's
advances awakens such fury In the breast
of the rejected one that the death of both
brothers is the outcome. Ryuro is not re
markable for his style, however, which is
inferior to that of the other two writers.
Surgeon General Mori Oliga Is a wntet
noted for his translations of several Ger
man works, and also for his original
short stories, .which are declared by all
Japanese to be excellent in style and con
ception. Another author, who is also
known as a translator, is Professor Hase
gama Shimel, a teacher In the Tokio For
eign Language School, and the only Jap
anese scholar who can he said tp be well
versed in Russian literature. He has
translated several of Tolstoi's and Tur
genieff's novels, and has produced two or
three original novels. His highly col
loquial style has done much : for the
simplification of the national written lan
guage.
Among other conspicuous novelists
should be mentioned Iwaya Sazanaml.
now a teacher of Japanese in the Berlin
Foreign Language School. He .Is the
only Japanese author, who has conde
scended to write stories for juvenile read
ers. Besides his adaptations from Lewis
Carroll and Grim, he has himself written
several popular children's stories.
Most Popular Japanese Author.
; Mural Gensai Is the most popular na
tive author in Japan â not. because- of hia
genius, however, but because of his busi
ness ability. In fact, he is a strictly "cash
author." His most popular work, "Ris
ing Sun Island," has been runnlnsr for
over three years in a daily paper. Its
chief merit in the eyes of the Japanese
is that it is strictly pure, clearly refers
to current affairs, and "Is tinctured with
general scientific facts made intelligible
to women." But the vast story, which
is of Interminable length, is simolv a
series of stories, loosely strung together.
It contains no character sketches worthy
of the name, no plot and no humorâ yet
20.000 copies of the first part of it have re
cantly.been sold, the sale being a record
one for Japan. This great success is
probably due to the fact that the com
parative purity of the story permits its
Introduction among women and families.
The story Is still running Its course In
the newspaper, and according to all ap
pearances may be running ten years
hence.

THE MARRIAGE CONTRACT.
SOMETHING like a mariia-for legislation on the
subject of matrimony has'possessed the legis
lators of the East during the past" winter. â¢ The
Call has repeatedly directed attention to variousbill^
designed to. encourage marriage by one means ; or
another, and it has now to refer to one 'designed tc
retard it a little; or at least to put an end-to one of
the forms in which it has been practiced or cmalprac
ticed to the injury of the community at large. â¢Â¦'- "^ .
California has had many notorious scandals and
several great lawsuits resulting from the ease with
which contract marriages can be effected under the
existing law. In New York, however, the situation
has been much* worse than in this State, for the popu
lation being larger the number of evils resulting from
lax marriage laws has Been proportionally greater.
Again and again the death of a rich man in that State
has been followed by the appearance of woman claim
ing to have been his wife by. a contract antedating his
public wedding to another. Wills have been contested
and estates wasted in prolonged lawsuits â¢ over such
claims, and not infrequently innocent women have had
to suffer the imputation of unlawful union with men
to. whom they ; were publicly wedded, and upon their
children has been fixed the. taint of illegitimacy. So
great have been the evils resulting from such cases
that families affected by them have sought to settle
them out of court at almost any cost, 1 and as a;conse :
quence the contract marriage has proven to be .a fruit
ful source of blackmail. V'^t;
For the purpose of putting an end to the frauds, and
affording protection to innocent women, the New. York
Legislature has adopted a law, to take effect January
i, which provides that no common law marriage con
tracted in the State on or after that date shall be valid
unless erttered into through a written contract of mar
riage signed by both parties and at least two witnesses,
who* shall subscribe to the same, stating the place of
residence of each" of the parties and witnesses, and
the place and date of marriage. The contract must
be acknowledged by the parties and witnesses. in the
same manner as a conveyance "of real estate, to enable
it to be recorded, and within six months after its exe
cution it must be filed in the office. of the clerk of the
town or city in which the marriage occurs. â¢
The new act changes what might be called a part
of the fundamental law of the State, for what are called
"common law marriages" have been recognized in
New York from; time immemorial. It is said by its
advocates to be the nios* important reform measure
passed by, the Legislature for many years' and is the
outcome of a prolonged agitation. It appears to merit
general approval, for it will n'pt only be a safeguard
for property .transmitted .by inheritance, but also a
protection for women, inasmuch as it is to their inter
est that the -fact oÂ£ la\yful .'marriage, should, be, always
certain and capable of proof./ ... *';
which yawns between East and West and
which is nowhere wider than in x literary
matters. I may add that if low living on
rice and fish, bathing in hot waterseveral
times a day and sitting spotlessly clean
on Immaculate ' mats reduces men to tne
condition of the novelists who allow them
selves to be trampled on like this, then i
am for dust, exercise, roast beef and beer.
What has really brought about this con
dition would be difficult to say. It may De
that the ; long isolation of the Japanese
and their subjection to the deadening in
fluence of China have made it difficult for
them to create all at once a noble litera
ture, but in process of time that literature
will come. On the other hand, it is as
serted that there must be some original
sin of weakness as well. Otherwise, as a
recent writer has noted, "the clash of
India and China with old mythological
Japan, of Buddhism with Shinto, of im
perialism with feudalism and of all with
Catholicism in the sixteenth century and
with Dutch ideas a little later, would have
produced more important results."
"Work of Translators.
Those who take this view should bear
in mind, however, that a nation cannot do
everything at once. A race may, indeed,
change its habits and customs and its na
tional policy rapidly, but It cannot so
quickly alter its cast of mind. If any ex
cuse can be offered for their pessimism it
lies in the apparent inability of many
highly educated Japanese to see through
European spectacles in matters of literary
taste. They get European books by trans
lationâand such translation! A Japanese
version of Bulwer Lytton'a "Ernest Mal
travers," which appeared in 1&79 under the
title of "A Spring Story of Flowers and
Willows," was the first European novel to
be translated, and it has been followed by
a torrent which never rushed more rap
idly than at the present moment. The
Japanese novelist â or translator I should,
perhaps, sayâ labors under the great dis
advantage of writing in a most difficult
and recondite system of symbols, which
has been compared, not without reason, to
much-abbreviated Latin telegrams. He
writes for a public, too, w-hlch cannot tell
when he is translating and when he is
original, and the result is that he borrows
plots wholesale from English and Amer
ican writers, merely Japanizlng the names
and altering the details to suit Japanese
social conditions. By this means he makes
a little money and becomes celebrated for
his fertility of invention, but ruins him
self irretrievably as an artist, if there
ever was the making of an artist in him.
It is only among the ignorant that he i3
celebrated; the educated Japanese hold
him In contempt and never read him.
Some Japanese Successes.
It may be regarded as a sign of the ex
tent of the translation folly that the hero
of the most popular Japanese work of fic
tion that has appeared during the present
reign isâ Epaminondas! This work, en
titled "Keikoku Bidan," takes the field of
Theban politics for its subject matter. Ihe
allusions were simply transferred withaut
much difficulty to modern Japanese poll-,
tics. Another successful novel, Kajln
no Kigu," has its opening scene laid in
the Capitol at Washington, where one of
the characters, a Japanese, reads aloud
to his companions the Declaration of In
dependence. Carlist pretenders, English
soldiers and other Europeans all appear
in the pages of this work,, which, by a
curious contradiction, is written in the
most classical Chinese style. An able
Japanese critic relates that- in 1895, "while
Japan was busy beating China, and had
convinced herself that she could. beat the
world, one of the Tokio papers, achieved
a success by the publication â¢ of a serltth
novel entitled 'Asahi Zakura.' The hero
ines of this book were two Red Cross
nurses, and the story that of the coming
defeat of England by Japan, which, after
annexing Hongkong, India, Malta and
Gibraltar, sends its fleet up the Thames
to raze the fortressess there and to ex
act from the cowering Britishers an
enormous indemnity."
One of Japan's Leading Novelists.
Novels of this class, of course, are not
fairly representative, although it is doubt
ful whether the European reader would
find satisfaction in many Japanese books
of distinctly better grade. Take, for ex
ample, "Tajyo Takon" ("Fears and Re
grets"), by Ozaki Koyo. one of the best
known of living Japanese novelists. The
central figure in this work is one Mr.
Sumi, "a graduate of the elective course
of geology" In the Tokio Imperial Univer
sity, who is earning a trifling wage as
teacher of ajcertain school of physics and
mathematics*. We find this man, when we
make his acquaintance, plunged In unut
terable grief on account of the loss of his
wife. Sumi weeps and groans, In fact, all
the way through the book, which contains
500 pages. There is a mother-in-law in
the book, Sumi's mother-in-law; this good
lady forces her second daughter to go and
live against her will with the dolorous
Sumi, whose household consists only of
himself and an old' female servant. The
heart-broken husband receives the girl.
T)Ut after some time sends a letter to the
Intriguing mother-in-law asking her to
take her daughter away. Now, Sumi has
a married friend called Hayama, but he
hates Mrs. Hayama â and the hatred is
returned, for the good woman' despises a
man who goes about shedding floods of
tears. The mutual dislike soon wears
away, however, on Sumi's taking lodging
in Hayama's attic, and each comes to es
teem the other, until "one rainy night the
recollection of his wife came too vividly
upen Sumi to be borne without Mrs. Hay
ama's soothing words and sympathetic
countenance" and he calmly proceeds to
make love to her. Hayama Is finally un
der the necessity of turning the tearful
lover out of doors. . -
One paragraph in the book, however,
may serve to give a hint of Japanese cyn
ical humor In fiction. Hayama, explain
ing why his friends fall off when he mar
ries, says:
Tou know, my : dear fellow, every married
woman secretly keeps a notebook. Neither her
husband nor her mother knows of its existence,
but it exists, all the same. It is called the
"Kltto O boye-cho" (book for keeping: things
well in the memory). She puts down in that
book, you see. the names of the Jolly old
bachelors who take her husband out; of the
fellows who happen to drop in. by the merest

Lot of the Japanese Novelist.
Even the globe-trotter- can see, however,
without making any investigation what
ever that the people read much. School
boys are constantly â¢: . found with paper
backed novels adorned on the cover with
a rude picturevrepresenting a Samurai
cutting someboc" r down; servant girls in
the teahouses devour; rather- highly spicea
"shotsetsu" ; in\ their scanty intervals of
leisure. I earned the undying gratitude
of one only yesterday by asking her to
continue reading in iny presence a love
story which Â¦ T, noticed in her hanging
sleeve while she was -bringing up my tif
fin. More remarkable still, that human
horse, â¢ the jinrikisha-man, has no sooner
set you down at your destination and
coiled himself up between the shafts to
await your return than he produces from
under the cane-bottomed seat orhis gro
tesque vehicle a ponderous tome that looks
like the work, of a Japanese Gibbon, but
is really only one of the many volumes in
which some popular, story-teller describes
the adventures of a favorite hero. â¢ Â¦
It should not be assumed from all this,
however, that the subjects of the Mikado
are all shining .examples of culture. Not
onlv are many of the books J generally
read distinctly bad in. character, but
s-trange as it may seem, eight readers out
of every tenpatronize verbatim shorthand
reports of professional story-tellers and
are therefore obviously dead to the sense
of literary felicity and artistic style.
Among the boys "detective" stories are
especially popular. Â¦ . ;" Â¦'Â¦"- " ;> .
Next in popularity to novels come tech
nical works, and of these medical books
nave the largest sale Of foreign works,
those in English head the list, six Eng
lish books being sold for every two in
German or French. Russian books have a
much smaller sale. The , sales of the
largest book store in the city do not ex
ceed 2500 yenâ less than $12oOâ a month,
but on account of the comparative pov
erty of the people and other reasons I
have given above, the number of readers
in the city must not be estimated from
these scanty figures.
Penny Dreadfuls inJapan.
In considering these writers the Occi
dental reader, must bear in i mind the pe
culiar conditions in which literature is pro
duced in Japan, the standing of the aver
age man of letters there and the taste of
the public to which he appeals. Take, as
an example, the public^ of Osaka, a thriv
ing city of 800,000 inhabitants, which in
some respects may be compared to such
an American city, say, as Chicago. There
are 'more works of fiction sold in Osaka
than of any other kind. It is estimated
that the sale of novels is 3509 a month, but
as this number does not include the vol
umes supplied by the circulatinglibraries,
which are said to' lend 'five times that
number of novels, it is safe to put the
demand for fiction in Osaka roughly at
20,000 volumes a month. This does not
seem to indicate a widespread taste for
reading, but when we take into considera
tion the fact that the Japanese are more
gregarious than Americans; that a dozen
Of .them will sleep in the same room as a,
matter of course where Europeans would
demand separate apartments, and that
there is no such thing as privacy in
Japan, it is safe to assume that one copy
Of a bookâ books, too,, are generally kept
cleaner in Japan than' elsewhereâ may"
pass through- the-hands of a hundred read
ers. Judging by the vast number of sec
ond-hand book stores all over the country
I should think, indeed, that single copies
of books go through more hands in Japan
than elsewhere and that .. the Japanese of
the lower class are astonishingly vora
cious : readers.. -Many of Fukuzana a
works, published immediately after the
Restoration, ran into half a million copies,
while of the late Mr. Nakamura's transla
tion of Smiles' "Self. Help" at least 300,000
copies must have been sold..;.,- ,
Owing to the energy and zeal with which
the entire nation has devoted itself in re
cent years to'trie' acquisition of the purely
material elements of Western civilization
modern Japan has impressed the Â¦world
rather by the increase of' its 'armaments
and the extension of its trade, than by ita
literature. Its surprising, material devel
opment, in fact, has obscured its growth
in other directions.- The leading Japanese
writers. oÂ£ to-day are almost unknown in
Europe and. America, yet these writers
comprise avconslderable and influential
body, and constitute a force that must
play a large ,part in determining Japan's
destiny. . ; ,' .

had he. then grouped half the sovereigns of Europe
around his hero for li>e purpose of giving. him a
greater prestige the painter would have been \ con
demned.
The Logan monument was intended to be of his
toric as well as artistic value,! and consequently aiiy
alteration of the facts of history by the sculptor is not
to be' justified. No particular harm has been done in
this case, but. it will be. well to have" it understood
that no 'such privilege is to be exercised by the de
signers of public monuments hereafter. It would be
a bad thing if after looking at a great work of art
purporting to represent some" scene in the history Gf
the nation the observer should,have to go and search
the files of newspapers to find out the facts 'of the
event. \. . .v>v . â¢ ;
AMONG the many conferences that are to be held
in connection with the Buffalo exposition,
.hardly any. promises to result in more direct
benefit to the nation than that which has been called
to take" into consideration the various taxation prob
lems of the time in this country. The problems have
been felt through the defects of the present systems
of taxation iri every State in the Union, and in. many
of them serious efforts have been made to devise reme
dies and^to provide for a more just and economical
way of collecting . from taxpayers the revenues re
quire'd for governmental purposes. '
The circular announcing the calling of the confer
ence and its purposes says: "For many- decades the
States have been, building up independent systems of
taxation, without reference to each other, until
now we have, a state of affairs .bordering on
chaos, where - each State is practically fighting
nearly. every.other State. Some property, is taxed three
or four times, while ether property is not taxed at
Tall.- Corporate activity has largely changed the char
acter of individual investments. Industry has over
stepped the boundaries cf any one State, and commer
cial'interests'are no longer confined to mere local
limits. This, conference will be the first attempt in
this country to work out some uniform principles." .
The statement that the conference "will be. the first
attempt in this country to work out some uniform prin
ciples" must have been intended to mean the first
attempt to bring about a uniform system of taxation
among the various States, for there have been many
efforts to find uniform principles of taxation generally.
At the present time the Industrial Commission is en
gaged in that very work, and has recently made public
a report upon the general property tax and the taxa
tion of corporations in several of the most important
States of the Union extending from Massachusetts to
Texas.
From a summary of the report sent out from Wash
ington it appears that in no State whose system of
taxation has been reviewed is the general property
tax satisfactory. The report says: "It is clear that
under this system there are numerous forms of wealth
that do not and cannot.be made to bear a just-share
of the public burdens, and which in a large part evade
or escape taxation, and some forms which when
reached at all under the prevailing general property
tax are not equally and uniformly but unjustly and
disproportionately taxed as: compared with other
property. The. inevitable result is that real estate and
some forms of personalty are unduly burdened with
taxation."- :â¢-..'
It is, of. course, not to be expected that:the Buffalo
conference, will itself Â«,olve any of the problems,in
volved in our confused systems of State taxation. It
may,- however, have the effect of bringing about the
appointment of something in the nature of an inter
state commission' to dealwith the complexities of the
subject. An \ absolutely uniform; system of taxation
among all the States is not to be looked for nor is 'it
desirable,. but enough might be accomplished to put
an -end to the trick by. which many corporations escape
taxation by. having their nominal headquarters in one
State, -doing business in several others" and shirking
taxes in all.
PROBLEMS OF TAXATION

MONDAY, APRIL 22.
The Slavonic Illyric Mutual Benevolent
Society of this city will give a picnic, on
Sunday, April 28, at Fernbrook Park, in
Nlles" Canyon, In conjunction with the
Slavonian-American Mutual Benevolent
Society of San Jose. The first named or
ganization was founded in this city in 1857
and numbers among its members many
leading citizens. A union band has been
obtained for the occasion and a large
number of prizes will be given. Antoine
Sorich is chairman of the committee of ar
rangements.
Slavonian Society Picnic.
a banquet at the California Hotel Satur
day evening. More than 100 members were
present and a -most enjoyable and,in
structive night was spent. Professor E.
E. Brown of the University of California
was the presiding officer and filled the
chair with ease and great satisfaction.
The principal speaker of the evening
was Senator Lukens of Alameda. He se
lected as the topic of his address "Recent
School Legislation." His speech was both
brilliant and logical and showed that he
was thoroughly conversant with the sub
ject. There were several other short
after-dinner speeches, which were warmly
received and loudly applauded.

the early Â¦ morning tides are given in the left

day In the order of occurrence as to time of
day; the third time column' gives the last tide
of the day, except when there are but three
tides, as sometimes occurs. The heights given
are in addition to the soundings on the United
States Coast Survey Charts, except whei. a
minus ( â ) sign .precedes the height, and then
the number given is subtracted from the depth
given by the charts. The plane of reference

of Host.
Captain W. G. Leale of the steamer
Caroline yesterday took a large number of
members of the Press Club and ladies on
a bay excursion, a most enjoyable trip be
ing the result. " ,
The Caroline was dressed from stem to
stern with bunting and flowers and the
guests were entertained with music, song
and refreshments. The trip included all
points of interest on the bay and a visit
was made to. San Quentin Prison. The
prison band serenaded the visitors and
guards were detailed to conduct the party
over the prison grounds. A welcome was
extended by Warden Aguirre. On the re
turn, trip the Caroline went to the Union
Iron Works, Adhere tno Wisconsin and
Ohio were Inspected. Hearty cheers were
given to Â¦ Captain Leale when his guests
landed at Jackson-street wharf In the
evening.- . - .

John Walsh of 243 Pacific street was
treated at the Karbcr .Hospital for gas
asphyxiation yesterday. He - says two
men entered his room and opened four of
the burners. He did not suffer very much
from the effects of the monoxide.
Sullivan said he had been visiting a
friend in the Mission and was on his way
lo his lodgings on Ellis street when two
men armed with revolvers suddenly con
fronted him and commanded him to throw
up his hands.
Realizing that they meant business, Sul
livan quickly obeyed the command. One
of the men then went through his pock
ets, securing his money and papers.. The
desperate pair then ordered their victim
to "move -on" under penalty of being
made a subject for the Coroner.
Sullivan lost no time in leaving the vi
cinity. The description of the robbers
tallies exactly with that given of the men
who held up a sailor on Howard street a
short time ago. The seafaring man was
walking along the street when he was
suddenly confronted bv two masked men,
who. after covering him with pistols, or
dered him to throw up his hands. Upon
his refusal to do so one of the robbers
shot him in the breast, inflicting a dan
gerous wound.
Edward Sullivan, an ex-soldier, reported
to the police yesterday that he had been
held up by two men on Eleventh street,
near Bryant, Saturday night, and robbed
of $100 and some valuable papers.

The bay schooner Lorenz and Â¦Mam
turned turtle in the bay yesterday . and
the captain and crew had a narrow es
cape. The schooner took on board a load
of coal, salved from the wreck of the bay
Flint, and started up the bay with it.
A squall struck the schooner and when
she heeled over the water rushed in and
turned her over. The coal all went back
to the bottom of the bav and the Lorenz
and William, with the captain andV his as
sistant perched on the side that was out
of the water, went drifting up -the. bay!
Off Hunters Point the vessel would have
gone ashore had not the State tug Gov
ernor Markham taken hold and anchored
her. The schooner not badly damaged
and to-day she will be righted and
brought to one of the wharves for repairs.

CAPSIZED IN THE BAY.
Schooner Lorâ¬nz and William Nearly
Goes to the Bottom.
THE dismasted German bark "Willy
Rickmers put into port yesterday
in distress. She -was caught in a
typhoon on March 5 and at 10 a.
m. the foretopmast, with every
thing attached, went by the boards. The
main and mlzzen topmasts soon followed
the foretopmast and then all hands had to
set to work cutting adrift the wreckage.
Owing to the heayv cross sea this was -a
particularly dangerous operation, but It
was finally accomplished without any
further mishap. . *
When the vessel was got under a jury
rig she labored heavily and in order to
save her more than 200 tons of the ballast
was jettisoned. This lightened her up
considerably and not only lessened the
strain but the bark sailed better. From
the time of the disaster until port was
reached the Rickmers averaged â¢ sixty
knots a day. the best run being 136 knots
in the twenty-four hours.
"We sailed from Hiogo, Japan, on Feb
ruary 23 last in ballast," said Captain J..
Baneke yesterday. "All went well for ten
days and then trie glass fell rapidly, so
the bark was put under snug- canvas. At
9 a. m. on March 5 it was blowing a-hur
ricane, with a very heavy cross sea. The
ship labored frightfully and every min
_ute I expected to see ; the masts roll out
of her. About 10 a. m. a heavy squall
struck the ship and the foretopmast with
everything attached went by the board.
The jet boom was sprung at the time and
one of the lifeboats smashed.
"The gale now increased in fury and
the v/ind shifted to every point of the
compass. First it came from the east and
an hour later was from the east-south
west, only to change to southwest and
then to west. The ship now began to roll
with sudden jerks and, the back stays
parting, the main topmast came down",
the mizzen topmasts with it. Some of the
yards and spars fell to port and some to
starboard, and as they came crashing
against the sides it looked at times as if
a hole would be punched in the hull. All
hands went to work and by 4 p. m. we had
ali the wreckage cut away. ,
"The yards that were left on the stumps
of the masts had nothing to hold them in
position and they went chopping around
! like the sails of a windmill. It was a
; dangerous operation to make them fast,
' but it was finally accomplished and -sve
got a little sail set. We had a rest for
the next forty-eight hours and then it
came on to blow again. A- big sea swept
; over the forecastle head and Cornelius do
, boer was carried overboard. He was' a
I good nvlmmer and as he fell to leeward
the chances were in his favor. The ship
I drifted right over him and when the poor
I fellow came ud to windward he seemed
! to have been injured, as he was swimming
Â¦ -with one arm only. "We threw him ropes
and lifebuoys but the wind drove them
back against the side of the ship and noth
' ing reached him. The unfortunate fellow
was only fifteen or twenty feet away from
the ship but, owing to the mountainous
sea and the strength of the wind, "we
could not lower a boat. De Doer was a
native of Holland and 24 years of age.
"After the second gale died out we had
nothing but fair weather to port and came
I along under our jury canvas at the rate
i of sixty knots a day. We were wrecked
in latitude 42 degree3 north, longitude 172
degrees east, and on March 29, in latitude
35 degrees north, longtitude 155 decrees
west, a four-masted schooner, the Rosa
mond, hove in sight. She passed about six
miles, to windward of us and could easily
have stood down, but the captain passed
us by as though there was nothing the
matter with us. On April 11. in latitude 38
degrees 10 minutes north, longitude 133 de
grees 50 minutes west, the J. C. Efluger
spoke us and the captain, I hear, reported
us all well. We are chartered to load
wheat at Portland, but I suppose that will
have to be abandoned. Until I hear from
the owners I do not know what will be
done with the vessel."
The "Willy Rickmers Is a four-masted
bark and was built in England in 1S95 for
the firm of Rickmers & Co. bhe '<Â« 1963
tons net burden, 279 feet long, 41 feet
beam and 24 feet 5 inches deep. She is
anchored In the stream near the German
bark Otto Gildemeister that was also dis
masted while on her way from Japan.

SAN FRANCISCO.
Games at Sacramento.
SACRAMENTO, April 21.-San Francis
co again defeated Sacramento to-day, the
score standing 7 to 3. TVhalen and Strick
lett filled the box and. while the latter
was touched up very lively in the fifth
and sixth innings, he put up a magnifi
cent exhibition for a sick man and fanned
five of the heavy stickers .from the bay.
He gave but two bases on balls and played
a heady game. Pabat made anÂ» unassisted
double on first, scored twice, had three
base hits, thirteen putouts and a clean
score in other xespects. Score:
Senators X.ose Three Out of Four

BLUE ROCKS. .
The shooting at the live-bird traps was
merely practice and secondary in import
ance to the bluerock. Phil Bekeart of
the San Francisco Gun Club annexed the
principal share of the honors by shoot
ing several clean scores.
The scores at the Hve-bird traps were:
Others .whose marksmanship merited
silver decoration were E. Klevesahl, C.'
A. Halght, F. Feudner (2), W. J. Golcher,
A. M. Shields, C. W. Debenham and J.
Ross of Antioch, who shot as the guest
of the club.
Otto Feudner carried off the honors of
the occasion by accomplishing fifty-four
straight killsâ a record which has not
been eaualed for many a day. Besides
this, he secured first prize in the mer
chandise contest and also two silver bars
with which to decorate his person.
The Olympic Club's offer to award gold
and silver bars to those who would suc
ceed during the open shoot at Ingleside
yesterday in breaking a certain number
of consecutive bluerocks brought , the
largest crowd of marksmen of the sea
son to the traps. Guns boomed continu
ally from morning to night. Over, 8000 of
the inanimate birds were shattered dur
ing the day, .which is the largest amount
by far . ever broken outside of a State
shoot. Both the live-bird traps and the
bluerock were liberally patronized. The
rewards offered by the club proved an
excellent Incentive, and as a result record
breaking scores Â¦were made.

Many Bluerocks Are Shattered and Pigeons
