ONE of the most successful enter
tainments ever given by the Cor
inthian Yacht Club took place on
Saturday in the clubhouse at Tib
uron. During the afternoon a re
ception was given to ladies, but after 6
p. m. the yachtsmen had the whole field
to themselves. The evening jinks took
the form of a minstrel show, In which
many old favorites took part.
Colors were made yesterday morning at
8 o'clock, and at 10 owners reported on
beard the flagship May. Shortly after 11
the fleet made sail, but the wind was so
light that for some time no progress was
made. Finding it too hard work to buck
the tide. Commodore Hawks led the way
up Raccoon Straits and to the north of
Angel Island. After passing Point Blunt
a gbod breeze was encountered, and the
fleet cruised for some distance along the
city front, then toward Sausalito and so
back to .the moorings. The only mishap
that occurred befell Frank A. Bartlett's
yawl Spray, which was under sail for the
first time since extensive alterations have
been made in her. While near the Ger
man ship Otto Gildermeister the jib car
ried away at its forward end, but was
soon repaired and the cruise resumed.
Among the yachts* following Commodore
H. D. Hawks' flagship May were the fol
lowing: J. M. Mattoon's sloop Amigo, A.
I. Lyons* sloop Freda, Dr. A. W. McKen
zie's sloop Sappho, P. W. Hurndall's sloop
Merope, Frank A. Bartlett's yawl Spray,
J. Wagner's yawl Nereid. J. Mooney's
sloop Belle, R H. Morrow's sloop
Rover, Vice Commodore E. F. Sa
gar's sloop Edna, J. W. Pew's sloop Tru
ant, A. Y. Young and T. Ransom's sloop
Clara, Jennings and Johns' sloop Speed
v/ell, Carl Westerfeld's sloop Aeolus,
Frank Stone's sloop Presto, "W. H.
Toepke's sloop Harpoon, Short brothers*
sloop Emma, Brickell and Bowes' slop
Genesta, J. A. Breitzmann's sloop Cupid,
Frank Baker's sloop Emilie, Andrew
Thome's yawl Seven Bells and Boyle and
Bulger's sloop Ariel. The fleet was ac
companied by the following . visiting
yachts: Commodore W. N. McCarthy's
schooner Ramona, Commodore S. S. Mar
shall's sloop Thelma, Allen M. Clay's
sloop Pactolus, J. T. Carrier's sloop Jessie
E, J. D. Spreckels' schooner Lurline,
Charles Stewart's sloop. Beatrice, D.
Dean's sloop Nellie, G. E. Billings' sloop
Nixie and the sloop Fawn. The yachts
Caprice, Vega and Discovery were also
under way. Commodore W. J. "Woods of
the sloop Angela came down from Vallejo
on Saturday to attend the jinks. Only two
Corinthian yachts now remain in the la
goon â W. S. Grover's yawl Arcturus and
the-sloop Phoenicia. A few'yachts are not
yet in commission, but will be made ready
as quickly as possible. Everything points
to a successful and pleasant yachting sea
son.

Another event of the morning game was
the presentation of a dozen bats to the
Oakland team. They were donated by the
management of the Dewey Theater,
which, in the enthusiasm of its joy over
the first appearance of the home team on
the home, grounds in the present season,
thought a few pennant-winning imple
ments would not be out of order. The bats
were accepted gracefully and with much
gratitude, and then the home team pro
ceeded to -lose its first' game to the Los
Angeles men by a score of 3 to' 6.- The
score in detail was as follows:'

LOS ANGELES.
Los Angeles made its only run in the
seventh. Kelly made second base on a
long hit to right field. Swindells hit to
third and was thrown out on tirst, Kelly
making third on the play and came home
on Bowman's hit to right field.
In the second inning Rellly turned his
foot in a dodging match between tirst and
second, and there was a general shifting
when he v^ent to the bench. Hartwell
came into the game to take Johnson's
place in the box, Johnson took Hutchin
son's place at first and Hutchinson took
Reilly's. place on third.
One questionable grenius in the sunny
part of the grand stand, just behind the
plate conceived the idea of dazzling the
pitcher's eyes with the aid of a mirror.
He tried it on Moskiman, but Moskiman
spotted him in short order and called the
umpire's attention to it. Pete Lohmari,
Oakland's captain, had his own way of
settling- it, however. He knew some one
who was sitting near the disturber and he
said for the benefit of the grand stand;
"He'll stop it if he is a gentleman. If he
doesn't, let him have it where he wants
it, Mac." Mac was the friend sitting near,
and as he showed a willing-ness to carry
out Lohman's idea there were no further
reflection* cast upon the pitchers.
In the third. Francks sent a short one to
third and reached first before the ball,
which was leisurely lielded. He stole sec
ond and then- made third 'and home on a
wild throw to second. In the fifth inning
Arrellanes made a run on a base on balls,
Streib's hit to right field and rmnleavy's
hit over in the same territory. Streib
was as far as the third bag when Moore
made the third out on a fly to center.
Babbitt made the last run for Oakland,
starting on a base on balls. He made

OAKLAND WINS
AND LOSES ONE
George Gardner and George Burns ham
mered one another through six rounds.
What they lacked in science they made
up in grit. Burns had the better of it the
last round and was awarded the decision.
The call of time saved Gardner from a
knockout.
Gus Brown and Ed Kelly went the limit,
six rounds, and though Kelly Was severely
punished, winding up with a broken rib,
he got a draw by his hard work in the
last round. Brown's Ions reach and cool
head were more than a match for Kelly's
aggressiveness.
The first bout, between Bert Fisher and
John McDonald, was short but fierce.
Early in the first round Fisher put Mc-
Donald on Queer street with a right jolt
to the mark, and put him down and out
with two left pokes to the jaw. -

these places Erne will go when he trains

Frank Erne did not stay long at the
training quarters of McGovern. He told
the latter he did not come out to see him
work, but just for a friendly call. Terry
and he shook hands as Erne drove off,
and then the former took a ride to - the
Cliff against wind and dust. Upon his re
turn he stripped and began his afternoon
work, which consisted of a four-round
bout with Dougherty and two rounds with
Dukelow. Terry put in the morning by
covering seven miles of park roads. This
Is his regular work and he shows up well
under it.
Over at Croll's Gardens there is an ear
nest little fellow who improves each min
ute of his time. Oscar Gardner is train
ingâthere is no mistaking it. At present
a cut lip keeps him from boxing, but he
does enough hard work to compensate for
the lack of cuffing â¢with his sparring part
ner. Gardner is in tip-top shape and
grows stronger every day of" his training.
He seems to be more than >earnest, too,
and will not for a moment give way to the
general public belief that McGovern is in
vincible. "Wait," he says. "I am going
to settle old scores this time. I think I will
come out oMhis fight the victor."
Gardner looks as if he meant what he
says. There is no doubt that he is train
ing hard, -and McGovern knows it. He
realizes it so completely that to-morrow
Terry will change his training quarters
from the Casino to the Terminal, just to
get away from distraction and visitors,
and work in a place where the "crowd
cometh not."
BUSS AND KENNEDY WIN
DOUBLES TOURNAMENT
They Defeat Gleason and Levy by
Two Points in Final Hand
. â¢ ball Game.
Russ and Kennedy defeated Gleason and
Levy yesterday in the final game of the
Olympic Club doubles handball tourna
ment by a score of 51 to 49. The game
was one of the most exciting- played in
the regulation court in many days. Russ
did. not, however, play up to his usual
form, and Gleason had little trouble re
turning his serve. Gleason was unused
to the ball, and did not return It to the
front wall with the accuracy for which he
is noted.
Kennedy and Levy surprised the experts
by their consistent playing. They were
in splendid condition and played to win.
At the outset of the contest Russ and
Kennedy secured a lead of 14 points. This
was lessened in time, and when the 30
mark was reached the score was very
close. Gleason and Levy evened matters
in the forties, and then passed their op
ponents. The game then took on a serious
aspect for Russ and Kennedy, and they
played hard to win out.
The score was 44 to 45 in their favor when
Levy went in to serve.' He sent a low,
swift toss to Russ, and made 49. He then
hit the side wall and went out. Gleason
then tried. He sent a swift one to Russ,
and it was returned to the wall. Gleason
lobbed the ball and it struck one of the
rafters, and he was out. 4
Kennedy then went in, and by a hard
serve won the game and first prize for
his team. ... '
Gleason and Levy will receive silver
medals. With the victors, they will re
ceive trophies for their skill on Wednes
day evening during the progress of the
wrestling tournament.

OSCAR GARDNER, at his training
quarters, Croll's Gardens, Ala
meda. and Terry McGovern, at the
Casino, the road resort north of
the park, were two very important
young pugilistic gentlemen all of yester
day forenoon and afternoon. At both
places crowds composed of the curious
who like to Jook upon the prizefighter
when he works in preparation for "the
event" gathered In numbers too great for
the peace and quiet of mind of the prin
cipals who will meet in the great battle
of featherweights on April 30. Gardner
had a busy time of it at Alameda, but his
was a Sunday-school session compared to
the tempestuous though informal recep
tion forced upon McGovern.
Of all the visitors that crowded and
blocked traffic at the Casino the most
welcome was Frank Erne. He came out
In a team on Saturday to' see Terry and
Manager Harris to talk about a prospec
tive fight in which he should figure as a
principal. The featherweight was at the
track at the time and Frank Erne re
turned to town a disappointed man. But
he met his game yesterday and one hour
after his arrival at the Casino he was in
deep-browed consultation with Sam Har
ris. Frank Erne left the Casino satisfied.
He says that, although no definite promise
of a match with McGovern was declared,
he is assured' by Harris' tone that he will
fight thÂ© Wonder and the Terrible some
time In June.
When they approached the Casino on
the return McGovern asked Erne where
he would train for his fight, if there
should be any. "At Sausalito or Alameda,"
was thjp ready response. "I want aÂ«place
where there is water â swimming, sailing,
Then the two kept silence for a time,
one resting on a retrospect of victory, the
other uneasy in a recollection of defeat.
The third party asked McGovern if he
would give Erne a chance for another go.
"Why, of coarse," said Terry. "If Har
ris gives the word, why, I'll right Shar
key."
, Along- the dust-blown rqad the two
trudged, perfectly at ease and communi
cative as two youngsters out of school.
They talked of training quarters, of fight
ers, of mutual friends and of the Califor
nia climate. Both agreed as to San Fran
cisco being a city in which there is life
and fun. They want to remain within its
confines for some little time and taste of
Its good things.
The meeting yesterday between McGov
ern and Erne was the first since last July,
when there was no talk but all fisticuffs,
somewhere in a roped ring: in New York
city. 'â¢Hello, Frank," "Hello, Terry" and
a handshake and the two were together
as if they had never crossed fists. "Let
us get away from the crowd," said Erne,
and Terry assented. Accompanied by a
Call representative. Erne and McGovern
strolled through the park. There was
nothing bellicose in the conversation be
tween these two noted pugilists.
"Hope you have a house something like
ours in New York last July," said Erne.
"Wasn't it great," was the McGovern
reply.

politan Hall this afternoon. His subject

The lecture will be under the auspices

marks Â¦will" prove of special interest to
children, and in order to give the little

ments have been made to secure^the early

WEST AT ELMHTTRST
Colonel Thomas McGregor of the Ninth
Cavalry arrived in the city Thursday from
Manila on the transport Lawton, teo he
will be able to be present at the games.
He has not missed an annual gathering
of the club for the past ten years, no mat
ter where he was stationed, and he was
determined that distance should prove no
obstacle to his attending â¢ this year. He
was at the meeting Friday night and was
accorded a hearty reception.
Communications were read from the
Caledonian clubs of Portland and Sacra
mento announcing that the members
would attend the games in a body, and
from the leading Scottish societies in the
East that they would send large delega
tions to the games to compete In the forty
events on the programme.
An open meeting of the Caledonian Club
was held in Scottish Hall Friday night
when an interesting programme of songs
and Instrumental music was rendered.
Afterward those present discussed the ap
proaching annual games, to be held In
Shell Mound Park May 30,, and various
suggestions were offered for making the
affair successful. A large sum was do
nated for the refreshment tent, which will
be under the charge of First Chieftain A.
McNair and a number of assistants, and
every guest will be properly looked after.
It was also announced that all trains from
the north would stop at the park on the
morning of the games and so would all
trains leaving for the north in the even-
Ing so as to accommodate visitors to the
games. . .

PBOMINENT ATHLETES
COMING FROM EAST
NEW YORK, April 21.â The World's
Paris cablegram says: Mount Vesuvius
is again in a state of active eruption and
many peopleâ scientists, students and the
curious, including American touristsâ are
leaving Paris by every train for Naples.
The first sign of disturbance was ob
served a week ago, when for three days
columns of smoke, issuing rapidly from
the crater in great volume, towered high
above the volcano. Then came occasional
rains of hot cinders, sometimes sprinkling
the country for several miles around.
Now lava is beginning to run.
The fire at the crater is very intense at
night, illuminating the surrounding re
gion beautifully.
Only two slight tremors of earthquake
have been felt so far.

Magnificent Sight

ets will be sold at $5 per chance. Here is
his proposition:
"I have been ten years in the country.
I am 28 years old. I worked hard, passed
the regrents' examination and entered Cor
nell Medical School. By working during
vacations, doing tutoring and other tasks,
I managed to support myself until I got
my doctor's diploma.
"Now I â¢want to marry, settle down and
begin practicing in a proper manner. I
have not the necessary money, so I
thought out the marrying plan by raffle.
This isimy scheme. There will be a few
necessary restrictions. They are three:
"The women must be between 20 and 30
years.'
"They must not be cripples or ainicted
with any incurable disease.
"The contest Is not open to negresses,
but there are no restrictions as to reli
gious belief or nationality.'
The doctor's picture is presented in to
day's World.

STOCKTON, April 21.â Orrin S.
Henderson, the Republican nom
inee for Mayor of Stockton, is at
present chairman .of the Board of
Supervisors and has been active
in political and commercial life for several
years. He is in his thirties and belongs
to the younger element of the city. He is
a Native Son and stands high In the Ma
sonic and other orders. He has been an
avowed candidate for Mayor for a year,
and won the nomination through the per
sistent work of a large personal following
which he has gathered around him dur
ing his public life. Henderson has confi
dence In himself, and this confidence is
shared by friends both in and out of his
political party.
George E. Catts. the Democratic nomi
nee for Mayor of Stockton, is a member
of the firm of Lauxen & Catts, and, like
his opponent, Henderson, is young in
years, but fully matured in the activities
of public life. While never holding politi
cal office, Catts has been before the public
in commercial bodies and on various pub
lic committees, wherein he demonstrated
his abilitr.
With two such capable young men
OFFERS CHANCE
TO OLD MAIDS
MISS GERTRUDE DTJTTON, "WHOSE RARE CHARM AND , FASCINAT
ING BEAUTY HAVE MADE HER A GENERAL FAVORITE IN SAN
FRANCISCO SOCIETY DURING THE PAST SEASON.
Somebody has been telling me things
again about the Century Club and about
a stormy meeting 1 , in which the Iadie3 ex
changed words, peppering their sentences
well with the thoroughly feminine but al
together unclubllke exclamations, such
as "Now there!" and ""Well, I don't
care!"
It was all on account of the coming big
reception and the very necessary adjunct
to aU successful entertainmentsâ the re
freshments. Mme. ' Chairman was for
having a certain caterer and the rest of
NEVKR before has a stranger come
within our gates and received
such genuine admiration and
marked social attentions as have
been showered upon Miss Agnes
Spreckels. It is hardly fair that I should
refer to Miss Spreckels as a stranger. She
is 'one of us, and it is only the fact that
she has been so long from us and intends
to' shortly again leave us that makes me
think of her as though she were a
stranger. While she is here with us, how
ever, we are all making the best of her
visitâ basking in her smile and enjoying
the rare charm, of her personality and
conversation. Agnes Spreckels is a woman
I would unhesitatingly, set down as a
beauty. She is tall and straight and
gives one the impression of inherent
physical strength. Her eyes are large and
of a soft, tender gray, and her pretty brown
hair frames a brow on which intellect is
graven in great letters. During her pro
longed stay in I>resden Miss Spreckels has
given much' of her time to study, showing,
however, a partiality for architecture. I
have been reflecting recently and have
caught myself wondering if it was not
the study of architecture, that love for
the strong and the big and the beautiful,
that has given to Miss Spreckels her
broad, generous views and her happy,
concise way of expressing them.
For those of us who have only met Miss
Spreckels at the formal affairs that have
been given in her honor I am sincerely
sorry. If I were one of them I should
maneuver to get an invitation to some
of those many little luncheons or dinners
at which she is honored. There they will
have the happy opportunity of seeing
Miss Spreckels at her best informal, gra
cious self.

Chinese Houses Empty.
Everything was dark around, the Chi
nese quarter last night, particularly in
those portions frequented by the' class of
women -who "were raided on Saturday. Of
ficers who were specially detailed to lo
cate them were unable to find any clew
to their whereabouts, though It ' is pre
sumed that ' they have gone to Oakland,
San Jose, Sacramento and other interior
points pending a cessation of the raids.
VALPARISO, April 21.â The Chilian
cabinet crisis came to an end to-day
when a new government was formed by
Senor Zanartu, who will take the depart
ment of the interior. Senor Luco is min
ister of foreign affairs and Senor Roari
guez minister of finance.
New Chilean Government.
pitted against each other, the campaign
will be close and exciting, but both will
strive to see that It is conducted in a fair
and Impersonal manner. Whoever is
elected, Stockton will have a young, vig
orous and energetic executive.

Then* is a decided lull in ihings theat
rical this week. The Columbia Theater
will be dark for two weeks, beginning to
night, owing to the canceling of James
K. Hackett's engagement, due to the ill
ness of Mr. Hackett. The theater will re
open on Monday, May 6. with James A.
Herne's new play, "Sag Harbor," exactly
as produced in New York. The Henry
Miller season will follow, and is an event
anticipated with heartiest interest among
the piay-goers. "Richard Savage" will be
Included in the Miller repertoire.
The Orpheum' s good bill is a light amid
the dramatic darkness. Miss Francesca
Redding repeats her success of last week
in the same brijrht playlet, "Her Friend
of Texas." and this week will be gowned
to distraction in a Parisian creation that
was designed originally by Felix for
Princess Helen ot Montenegro. The gown
has a ourious history. It was ordered by
Princess Helen shortly before thÂ« assassi
nation of King Humbert of Italy, and the
order was countermanded, by reason of
the throwing of the courts of Europe into
mourning for the King's untimely death.
The dress was thus left on Felix's hands,
and was exhibited by him at the Paris
Exposition as a sample of the fine art of
gown-making-. There Miss Redding found
xt and, though it cost her several times
her fare to the exposition, she thinks it
was worth the cost. Beside Miss Redding
end the Felix gown there are Barnes and

Grant, Madame Marion von Sehrader.

The Alcazar has a strong attraction for

.tt new play by Paul M. Potter, that has

production at the Empire Theater. The
play is historical in origin, dating back to
l.he battle of Sedan. The scene of the play
Ip laid in Britanny and the plot deals -with
the events consequent upon the occupa
tion I"" the Prussians cf the castle of
Grand Pre.

Frank Hess Arrives In i This City
From Ohio in Search of His
. Cousin. . - .
Frank Hess. a\ young man agred ,19
years, lately from -Ohio, is anxious to
find his cousin. Frank B. Johnson, who
is. supposed to be somewhere. in this city.
Johnson, who is a bricklayer by trade,
is one of the heirs- to a fortune- left by
Hess' mother, who died on the 19th inst.,
leaving an estate valued at $40,000 to her
son and nephew. â¢ v .
v Young Hess is- anxious to find his
cousin, so .they, may return to Ohio-arid
take possession of ' the property.
BRICKLAYER JOHNSON.
HEIR TO A FORTUNE
The volunteers in camp throughout the
Presidio and the regular troops garrison
ing the post were greatly pleased at. the
reopening of the Red Cross tent yester
day, and s.howed their appreciation of the
efforts of the ladies in their behalf by
crowding the canvas structure.
Magazines, newspapers and story books
of all kinds were eagerly grasped by the
soldiers. Most of the men prefer a quiet
spot and a periodical to read to spending
their time roaming aimlessly about the
camp. The supply of reading matter in
the hands of the ladies having the tent in'
charge is ample. Their intention is to se
cure a piano and a collection of all sorts
of games to furnish amusement for the
soldiers. Maps and atlases are greatly in
demand, ; and dictionaries are greatly
needed. ,Â¦ Â¦ ' \ . >
The tent will be supplied with comport
able chairs, desks and tables, will be
lighted by electricity, and be open from
10 a. m. to 10 p.m. . Chaplain Miller of the
Presidio has i secured it for one night a
week and will endeavor to provide enter
tainment for the men.
There are at present 9000 soldiers at the
Presidio, .and the . reservation, presents a
lively aspect. Hundreds of visitors spend
hours there daily, and the scene is not un
like the appearance of the reservation at
the outbreak of the war. Six regiments
of volunteers are quartered in the camps
and the men appear happy and contented.
They will remain for at least three weeks,
as> the force employed in preparing the
finals > will require that time to prepare
them for the mustering out process.

bought the flask of whisky. He was then
under the influence of liquor. The body
was taken to the Morgue.
Steinhagen was a bachelor. 52 vears of
age. and a native of Germany. He was
at one time a prosperous commission mer
chant, but in recent years had done a
Email business and had become de
spondent.

The city is in complete readiness to re
ceive the Grand Parlor delegates. Not
since the palmy days of floral festivities
has it been so profusely decorated. State
street is illuminated with hundreds of col
ored lights and flag lines make a good
showing by day.
The Grand Parlor will hold its first ses
sion to-morrow morning, and arrange
ments, have been made for a drive over
the mountain boulevard in the afternoon.
In the evening there will be a reception
at Grace M. E. Church. The delegates
appear to be a fine aggregation of repre
sentatives, and . the most interesting
Grand Parlor for many years is expected.
Some j)f the Â¦ candidates for office have
been here since yesterday and there has
been some wire-pulling. It is generally
conceded that Frank Coombs will be the
next grand president/but there will be a
fight between S. A. Jones and Lunstedt
for the secretaryship.
SANTA BARBARA, April 2Lâ The Na
tive Sons arrived at 9:30 o'clock on the
special train this, evening. They were
somewhat travel worn, but reported a fine
trip. At Paso Robles they stopped an
hour and a half for luncheon and saw
the town. The special also stopped at
nearly every other place along the line.
There was a big 'crowd at Victoria sta
tion . to meet the delegates this evening,
and arrangements were made for lodg
ings. The city is full of tourists already,
ana it has kept the reception committee
busy to find accommodations for all.
The overland from Los Angeles with
the southern delegates aboard is several
hours late and will not reach here before
midnight. The private car of President
Hays of the Southern Pacific Is attached
to it. . Â¦_ - . Â¦ ,

known, and the "old push" can only
conjecture how the ballots will fall next
Wednesday.

enjoyed the various features at the
Chutes. The Chutes theater bill for this
week promises to be exceptionally good

Iiively Buzz of Politics.
However auiet the trainload of Native
Sons may have been to outward appear
ances, it was a-buzz with political ex
citement of the Native Son sort. If two
delegates shook hands across -the aisle
conversation ignored the weather and
went at once to ; "politics." "How are
your fellows standing on the fight for
grand orator?" "Whom do the country
members want for grand trustees?" "It
looks as if it were Lunstedt agraln, don't
it?" These were the sort of preliminaries
that soon had six carloads of . "natural
born" politicians humming like beehives
of a spring morning. ~ .'Â¦'..-
Interest mainly centers in the contest
for grand orator. Superior Judge Dooling
of San Benito County and Superior Judge
McLaughlin of Plumas County are the
candidates, and. not a delegate can be
found who will' claim a victory for either.
The two Judges are great personal
friends, and the contest has nothing in it
foreign to fraternal feeling, but a fight
of the liveliest sort is beine made of- it.
S. A. D. Jones of Pacific Parlor, late of
Pomona, is in the field for grand secre
tary, but the consensus of opinion is that
Henry Lunstedt will be re-elected hands
down.
Of course. Grand First Vice President
Frank L. Coombs, Grand Second Vice
President Lewis F.' Byington and Grand
Orator McNoble will move up the official
line in regular order. The office of grand
marshal will go to the town which gets
the 9th of September celebration. Sacra
mento will very probably be thait^town.
MEEK, subdued and shivering in
the "cold, gray dawn," 200 del
egates to the Grand Parlor of
the Native Sons left by special
train at 7:30 o'clock yesterday
morning. An unusually large number of
them were accompanied by their wives,
and many enthusiastic members of the or
der who were not delegates -were aboard
the train, so that when the special pulled
out from the Third and Townsend streets
depot its six coaches carried fully 300
people.
The early hour, the chill air and the
taste in the mouths of the delegates of
the high jinks provided for them the
night before by the Past Presidents' As
sociation precluded any particular show
of enthusiasm- Delegates from parlors
all over Northern and Central California
hurried to the depot, hunted out their
particular friends in the coaches and
quietly settled themselves for as com
fortable a ride as possible, content to let
the fireworks of enthusiasm wait until
some such thing as delegations of Native
Daughters at Pajaro bearing tribute of
luscious strawberries and mellow apples
should furnish the spark to set them.go
ing.
A large number of the celebrities of the
order were aboard the special train. Hen-
George L. Jones of Quartz Parlor, Grass
Valley; A. M Smith of Argonaut Parlor,
Oroville, and William Wagoner of Arrow
head Parlor, San Bernardino, are among
the other candidates for grand trustee.
As to results, everybody is guessing.
Sixty-five per cent of the country dele
gates are new men, never before in at
tendance at a Grand Parlor, in official ca
pacity at least, and the old-timers can
not place them, j Their likes and dislikes
From outside of San Francisco Grand
Trustees Radcllff and Knowland are can
didates for re-election. McLaughlin and
Dooling having chosen - to fight it
out for grand orator. Knowland of
Alameda and Radcliff of Watsonville
have both made excellent records
as members of the State Assem
bly, and as both are popular in
the order, their re-election is regarded as
assured. Radcliff resurrected the Big
Basin Redwood Park bill, a pet measure
of the Native Sons, after it had been done
to death in committee, and his persistent
efforts were mainly responsible for the
final success of that measure, Knowland
fathered several important educational
bills and the famous Chinese slavery bills,
and was chairman of the committee that
investigated the San Francisco police
scandals. . .
Candidates From. Interior.
Candidates for grand trustee are
legion. By tradition four of the seven
will be chosen from the country parlors
and three , from San Francisco parlors.
Percy V. Long. Will D. Shea and James
L. Gallagher of San Francisco are candi
dates for re-election. In addition. Stephen
\. Costello of Pacific Parlor. Senator J.
H. Nelson of San Francisco Parlor,
Thomas E. Curran of Dolores Parlor and
Major Boxton of National Parlor are can
didates. "
So far, Santa Cruz is the only rival of
the capital city for that honor. Vallejo
wants the next Grand Parlor, and ap
parently is slated for it, though the San
Francisco delegates may decide that the
metropolis needs the plum.
J. Emmet Hayden of Mt. Tamalpais
Parlor of San Rafael is a candidate for
grand outside sentinel.
,More successful than the greatest ex
pectations of its managers was the Vere
in Eintracht fair, which closed last night
with a grand ball at the hall on Twelfth
street, near Folsom. It is expected that
more than $2000 'will -be.- realized for the
fund for renovating the building, to
which purpose will be devoted the net
proceeds. ,--. - , Â¦Â¦.--.
U It was a merry throng that gathered
about the booths, had Â¦â¢ fortunes told,
danced, â¢ ate. Frankfurters and drained
copious steins, all in aid of the cause.
One of the â¢ most interesting features
was the voting contest. A score of young
ladies were entered . in '.the lists, and at
a. late hour the counting of Â¦ ballots was
commenced. 'The leaders for the prize of
beauty were Miss F. Hays and Miss E.
Kaufman. In the contest for the most
popular,, the same relative, positions
were held by Miss E. Schernstein and
Miss A. Gerichten. Each young lady's
partisans -watched the count with eager
interest, and so great was the balloting
toward the close that at a very late hour
all the votes had not been counted.
The evening's programme embraced
choruses by the German singing sections,
turning by members of the Oakland Turn
Verein, glees by the Alpenrosll, . exhibi
tions by the ladies' turn sections, : mass
choruses by vocalists from all . German
organizations, concluding with classic
pyramids by the turn section of the Ein
tracht Verein.
Finally came the dance, and the closing
hours of the fair were whirled away to
the merry strains of waltz," polka, schot
tische and quadrille.

BIG -FINANCIAL SUCCESS
Retail Cigar Dealers Organize.
An organization of retail cigar dealers
has recently been formed in this city, by
which they seek to protect their interest
against undercutting in prices. A com
mittee was appointed, consisting of J. N.
Gregory, M. ';oblentz. H. IC. Riblet and H.
Kupey. This body will wait on all dealers
with the view of having them agree on a
fixed price at which cigars shall be sold
by retailers. This move is aimed at such
dealers as trade in Chinese-made cigars
and who can afford to undersell the hand
lers of Eastern-made goods.
More than 200 proprietors of 10-cent bar- I
ber shops met in B'nai B'rith Hall yester
day afternoon and formed the Boss Bar
bers' Protective Union. Organization was
effected by the election of L. E. Conrad.
president: M. J. Silva. secretary; Sigmund !
Stearns, treasurer, and Samuel Oppen
heimer. sergeant-at-arms.
Representatives from the Journevmen \
Barbers' Union were present, and there
was a friendly discussion, lasting several
hours, as to what prices should be
charged. The rates on hair-cutting and j
hair-singeing ic was agreed to raise to 25 I
cents. Whether sea foams should be
charged for at the rate of 10 or 15 cents
vas decided in favor rf the lower price.
Whether to charge 35 cents each day for
a shav/> or 25 cents on Sundays and "holi
days was the greatest and most difficult
question to solve. The smaller barbers
urged 15 cents straight for seven days a
week, and carried their point by a decisive
vote on division.
In order to fully discuss the matter with
the Journeymen Barbers' Union a com
mittee, consisting of Lehman, Levy. Ober
ly. Heller and Silva, was appointed to
meet its delegates to-night and report -
back to the parent body at a meeting to
be held on Tuesday night. If the rates
and other arrangements are satisfactory
they will be adopted formally on Tuesday
night.
.Andrew Lindberg, residing .at 115 Milton
street, had a lacerated wound on his heao
dressed at the City and County Hospital
yesterday, which was inflicted by his son
Conrad. Father and son had a disagree
ment over, a domestic matter while at
-dinner and the son picked up a dish and
threw it at his parent, - cutting a deep
gash in his forehead.
Assaulted by His Son.
George Lods says that he has always
been willing to work and that he has now
coming to him a week's wages, from the
Humboldt Lumber Company. He says
he has not worked steadily but that he
has earned his own living and has'paid
his family $4 a week. Latterly, he says,
he has split this up and has paid $2 to his
mother and;J2 to his father.
The son tells a different story. He says
his father has been in the habit of strik
ing his mother and that yesterday morn
ing he struck her twice. He suggested
that she go into another room, but he
says his father followed him and ordered
him from the house. He says the old man
pulled down the folding bed in which he
slept and proceeded to dismantle it and
then went for his revolver. There was a
fight and the father was wounded. The
Bon says the father was the one who
brought out the revolver.
Charles Lods, the father, has been head
carpenter of -the Spring Valley "Water
Company for thirty years. He says he
has repeatedly secured positions for his
son and that he has had him working in
various places, but he. would not keep
any of them. He says tie came home yes
terday and upbraided the young man for
his idleness and that his son went over
to. where a revolver was kept and at
tacked him with it. There was a scuffle
for the possession of the weapon and fath
er and son rolled over the floor in the
struggle. Mrs.. Lods was a witness to it
all and she seized a mallet and joined in
the flg-ht. She struck George a heavy blow
on the head, which weakened him, and as
he loosened his hold on the revolver it
was discharged. The bullet entered the
right thigh of the old man, pierced it and
struck again in .the calf of the leg. It
went nearly through again and lodged
under the skin near the ankle, where a
surgeon at the Emergency Hospital found
it and cut it out. The young, man was
arrested on a charge of assault to mur
der. ;
There has ljeen bad blood between
Charles Lods of .120 Diamond street and
his son George for some time, the father
laying it to the idleness of his boy and
the son to the disposition of the father.
The father says his son will not work
and | the son says his father drinks and
strikes his mother, and so the trouble has
been brewing: until it culminated in a
shooting affray. ' . :
In the presence of the wife and mother
yesterday a father and son fought for the
possession of a revolver and tne fight
ended only, when the weapon was dis
charged and the father was sorely wound
ed. It was fortunate that one or the other
was not killed. '
